Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevators&amp;redirect=no" title="Space elevators">Space elevators</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg"><img alt="Diagram of a space elevator. At the bottom of the tall diagram is the Earth as viewed from high above the North Pole. About six earth-radii above the Earth an arc is drawn with the same center as the Earth. The arc depicts the level of geosynchronous orbit. About twice as high as the arc and directly above the Earth\'s center, a counterweight is depicted by a small square. A line depicting the space elevator\'s cable connects the counterweight to the equator directly below it. The system\'s center of mass is described as above the level of geosynchronous orbit. The center of mass is shown roughly to be about a quarter of the way up from the geosynchronous arc to the counterweight. The bottom of the cable is indicated to be anchored at the equator. A climber is depicted by a small rounded square. The climber is shown climbing the cable about one third of the way from the ground to the arc. Another note indicates that the cable rotates along with the Earth\'s daily rotation, and remains vertical." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="697" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="604" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg/260px-Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg/390px-Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg/520px-Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_elevator_structural_diagram--corrected_for_scale%2BCM%2Betc.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A space elevator is conceived as a cable fixed to the equator and reaching into space.  A counterweight at the upper end keeps the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> well above geostationary orbit level. This produces enough upward <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> from Earth\'s rotation to fully counter the downward gravity, keeping the cable upright and taut.  Climbers carry cargo up and down the cable.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:260px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:518px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Space_elevator_in_motion_viewed_from_above_north_pole.ogv/518px--Space_elevator_in_motion_viewed_from_above_north_pole.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:518px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="56.122630385488" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Space_elevator_in_motion_viewed_from_above_north_pole.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5c/Space_elevator_in_motion_viewed_from_above_north_pole.ogv/Space_elevator_in_motion_viewed_from_above_north_pole.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; A free-flying satellite (green dot) is shown in geostationary orbit slightly behind the cable.</div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>space elevator</b> is a proposed type of planet-to-space transportation system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>  The main component would be a cable (also called a <a href="/wiki/Space_tether" title="Space tether">tether</a>) anchored to the surface and extending into space. The design would permit vehicles to travel along the cable from a planetary surface, such as the Earth\'s, directly into space or orbit, <a href="/wiki/Non-rocket_spacelaunch" title="Non-rocket spacelaunch">without the use of large rockets</a>. An Earth-based space elevator would consist of a cable with one end attached to the surface near the equator and the other end in space beyond <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary orbit</a> (35,786&#160;km altitude). The competing forces of gravity, which is stronger at the lower end, and the outward/upward centrifugal force, which is stronger at the upper end, would result in the cable being held up, under tension, and stationary over a single position on Earth.  With the tether deployed, climbers could repeatedly climb the tether to space by mechanical means, releasing their cargo to orbit.  Climbers could also descend the tether to return cargo to the surface from orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The concept of a tower reaching geosynchronous orbit was first published in 1895 by <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>  His proposal was for a free-standing tower reaching from the surface of Earth to the height of geostationary orbit. Like all buildings, Tsiolkovsky\'s structure would be under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compression_(physical)" title="Compression (physical)">compression</a>, supporting its weight from below. Since 1959, most ideas for space elevators have focused on purely <a href="/wiki/Tension_(physics)" title="Tension (physics)">tensile</a> structures, with the weight of the system held up from above by centrifugal forces. In the tensile concepts, a <a href="/wiki/Space_tether" title="Space tether">space tether</a> reaches from a large mass (the counterweight) beyond geostationary orbit to the ground. This structure is held in tension between Earth and the counterweight like an upside-down <a href="/wiki/Plumb_bob" title="Plumb bob">plumb bob</a>.\n</p><p>To construct a space elevator on Earth, the cable material would need to be both stronger and lighter (have greater <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">specific strength</a>) than any known material. Development of new materials that meet the demanding specific strength requirement must happen before designs can progress beyond discussion stage. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">Carbon nanotubes</a> (CNTs) have been identified as possibly being able to meet the specific strength requirements for an Earth space elevator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCfuture_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCfuture-4">[4]</a></sup> Other materials considered have been <a href="/wiki/Boron_nitride_nanotube" title="Boron nitride nanotube">boron nitride nanotubes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanothread" title="Carbon nanothread">diamond nanothreads</a>, which were first constructed in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCIAM_DN_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCIAM_DN-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xtech_DN_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xtech_DN-6">[6]</a></sup>.  In 2018 single-crystal <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">Graphene</a> was also proposed as a potential material<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>.\n</p><p>A prototype was launched in 2018 to tether to future stations as well as the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> It is a miniature version to be further examined before making the decision to build up a large structure in the coming years. \n</p><p>The concept is applicable to other planets and <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">celestial bodies</a>. For locations in the solar system with weaker gravity than Earth\'s (such as the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>), the strength-to-density requirements for tether materials are not as problematic. Currently available materials (such as <a href="/wiki/Kevlar" title="Kevlar">Kevlar</a>) are strong and light enough that they could be practical as the tether material for elevators there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Early_concepts"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early concepts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">20th century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#21st_century"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">21st century</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#In_fiction"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">In fiction</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Physics"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Physics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Apparent_gravitational_field"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Apparent gravitational field</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Cable_section"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cable section</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Cable_material"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Cable material</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Structure"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Structure</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Base_station"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Base station</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Cable"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cable</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Climbers"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Climbers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Powering_climbers"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Powering climbers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Counterweight"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Counterweight</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Launching_into_deep_space"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Launching into deep space</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Extraterrestrial_elevators"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Extraterrestrial elevators</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Construction"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Construction</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Safety_issues_and_construction_challenges"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Safety issues and construction challenges</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Economics"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Economics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#International_Space_Elevator_Consortium_(ISEC)"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">International Space Elevator Consortium (ISEC)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Related_concepts"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Related concepts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_concepts">Early concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tsiolkovsky.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="261" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Tsiolkovsky.jpg/170px-Tsiolkovsky.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Tsiolkovsky.jpg/255px-Tsiolkovsky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Tsiolkovsky.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tsiolkovsky.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The key concept of the space elevator appeared in 1895 when <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> scientist <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a> was inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>. He considered a similar tower that reached all the way into space and was built from the ground up to the altitude of 35,786 kilometers, the height of <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASASci_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASASci-10">[10]</a></sup> He noted that the top of such a tower would be circling <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> as in a geostationary orbit. Objects would attain horizontal velocity as they rode up the tower, and an object released at the tower\'s top would have enough horizontal velocity to remain there in geostationary orbit. Tsiolkovsky\'s conceptual tower was a compression structure, while modern concepts call for a <a href="/wiki/Tensile_structure" title="Tensile structure">tensile structure</a> (or "tether").\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Building a compression structure from the ground up proved an unrealistic task as there was no material in existence with enough compressive strength to support its own weight under such conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBIS1999_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-JBIS1999-11">[11]</a></sup> In 1959 another Russian scientist, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuri_N._Artsutanov" title="Yuri N. Artsutanov">Yuri N. Artsutanov</a>, suggested a more feasible proposal. Artsutanov suggested using a geostationary <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> as the base from which to deploy the structure downward. By using a <a href="/wiki/Counterweight" title="Counterweight">counterweight</a>, a cable would be lowered from geostationary orbit to the surface of Earth, while the counterweight was extended from the satellite away from Earth, keeping the cable constantly over the same spot on the surface of the Earth. Artsutanov\'s idea was introduced to the Russian-speaking public in an interview published in the Sunday supplement of <i><a href="/wiki/Komsomolskaya_Pravda" title="Komsomolskaya Pravda">Komsomolskaya Pravda</a></i> in 1960,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artsutanov_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-artsutanov-12">[12]</a></sup> but was not available in English until much later. He also proposed tapering the cable thickness so that the stress in the cable was constant. This gave a thinner cable at ground level that became thickest at the level of geostationary orbit.\n</p><p>Both the tower and cable ideas were proposed in the quasi-humorous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daedalus_(Ariadne)" title="Daedalus (Ariadne)"><i>Ariadne</i> column</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i>, December 24, 1964.\n</p><p>In 1966, Isaacs, Vine, Bradner and Bachus, four <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> engineers, reinvented the concept, naming it a "Sky-Hook", and published their analysis in the journal <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)"><i>Science</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> They decided to determine what type of material would be required to build a space elevator, assuming it would be a straight cable with no variations in its cross section, and found that the <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">strength</a> required would be twice that of any then-existing material including <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>.\n</p><p>In 1975 an American scientist, <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Pearson" title="Jerome Pearson">Jerome Pearson</a>, reinvented the concept yet again, publishing his analysis in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Acta_Astronautica" title="Acta Astronautica">Acta Astronautica</a></i>. He designed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pearson_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-pearson-14">[14]</a></sup> a tapered cross section that would be better suited to building the elevator. The completed cable would be thickest at the geostationary orbit, where the tension was greatest, and would be narrowest at the tips to reduce the amount of weight per unit area of cross section that any point on the cable would have to bear. He suggested using a counterweight that would be slowly extended out to 144,000 kilometers (89,000 miles), almost half the distance to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> as the lower section of the elevator was built. Without a large counterweight, the upper portion of the cable would have to be longer than the lower due to the way <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> and centrifugal forces change with distance from Earth. His analysis included disturbances such as the gravitation of the Moon, wind and moving payloads up and down the cable. The weight of the material needed to build the elevator would have required thousands of <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> trips, although part of the material could be transported up the elevator when a minimum strength strand reached the ground or be manufactured in space from <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">asteroidal</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_resource_utilization" title="In-situ resource utilization">lunar ore</a>.\n</p><p>After the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes" title="Carbon nanotubes">carbon nanotubes</a> in the 1990s, engineer David Smitherman of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>/Marshall\'s Advanced Projects Office realized that the high strength of these materials might make the concept of a space elevator feasible, and put together a workshop at the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a>, inviting many scientists and engineers to discuss concepts and compile plans for an elevator to turn the concept into a reality.\n</p><p>In 2000, another American scientist, <a href="/wiki/Bradley_C._Edwards" title="Bradley C. Edwards">Bradley C. Edwards</a>, suggested creating a 100,000&#160;km (62,000&#160;mi) long paper-thin ribbon using a carbon nanotube composite material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html-15">[15]</a></sup> He chose the wide-thin ribbon-like cross-section shape rather than earlier circular cross-section concepts because that shape would stand a greater chance of surviving impacts by meteoroids. The ribbon cross-section shape also provided large surface area for climbers to climb with simple rollers. Supported by the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Institute_for_Advanced_Concepts" title="NASA Institute for Advanced Concepts">NASA Institute for Advanced Concepts</a>, Edwards\' work was expanded to cover the deployment scenario, climber design, power delivery system, <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">orbital debris</a> avoidance, anchor system, surviving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_oxygen" title="Atomic oxygen">atomic oxygen</a>, avoiding lightning and hurricanes by locating the anchor in the western equatorial Pacific, construction costs, construction schedule, and environmental hazards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>To speed space elevator development, proponents have organized several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Elevator_Competitions" title="Space Elevator Competitions">competitions</a>, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Ansari_X_Prize" title="Ansari X Prize">Ansari X Prize</a>, for relevant technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Among them are <a href="/wiki/Elevator:2010" title="Elevator:2010">Elevator:2010</a>, which organized annual competitions for climbers, ribbons and power-beaming systems from 2005 to 2009, the Robogames Space Elevator Ribbon Climbing competition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> as well as NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Challenges" title="Centennial Challenges">Centennial Challenges</a> program, which, in March 2005, announced a partnership with the Spaceward Foundation (the operator of Elevator:2010), raising the total value of prizes to US$400,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\nThe first European Space Elevator Challenge (EuSEC) to establish a climber structure took place in August 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, "the <a href="/wiki/LiftPort_Group" title="LiftPort Group">LiftPort Group</a> of space elevator companies announced that it will be building a carbon nanotube manufacturing plant in <a href="/wiki/Millville,_New_Jersey" title="Millville, New Jersey">Millville, New Jersey</a>, to supply various glass, plastic and metal companies with these strong materials. Although LiftPort hopes to eventually use carbon nanotubes in the construction of a 100,000&#160;km (62,000&#160;mi) space elevator, this move will allow it to make money in the short term and conduct research and development into new production methods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Their announced goal was a space elevator launch in 2010. On February 13, 2006 the LiftPort Group announced that, earlier the same month, they had tested a mile of "space-elevator tether" made of carbon-fiber composite strings and fiberglass tape measuring 5&#160;cm (2.0&#160;in) wide and 1&#160;mm (approx. 13 sheets of paper) thick, lifted with balloons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Elevator:2010" title="Elevator:2010">Elevator:2010</a> held the 2007 Space Elevator games, which featured US$500,000 awards for each of the two competitions, ($1,000,000 total) as well as an additional $4,000,000 to be awarded over the next five years for space elevator related technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> No teams won the competition, but a team from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a> entered the first 2-gram (0.07&#160;oz), 100-percent carbon nanotube entry into the competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>  Japan held an international conference in November 2008 to draw up a timetable for building the elevator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapanUKTimes_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-JapanUKTimes-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2008 the book <i>Leaving the Planet by Space Elevator</i> by Dr. Brad Edwards and Philip Ragan was published in Japanese and entered the Japanese best-seller list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leaving_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leaving-30">[30]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> This led to Shuichi Ono, chairman of the Japan Space Elevator Association, unveiling a space-elevator plan, putting forth what observers considered an extremely low cost estimate of a trillion yen (&#163;5 billion / $8 billion) to build one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapanUKTimes_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-JapanUKTimes-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Obayashi_Corporation" title="Obayashi Corporation">Obayashi Corporation</a> announced that in 38 years it could build a space elevator using carbon nanotube technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> At 200 kilometers per hour, the design\'s 30-passenger climber would be able to reach the GEO level after a 7.5 day trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> No cost estimates, finance plans, or other specifics were made. This, along with timing and other factors, hinted that the announcement was made largely to provide publicity for the opening of one of the company\'s other projects in Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, the International Academy of Astronautics published a technological feasibility assessment which concluded that the critical capability improvement needed was the tether material, which was projected to achieve the necessary strength-to-weight ratio within 20 years. The four-year long study looked into many facets of space elevator development including missions, development schedules, financial investments, revenue flow, and benefits. It was reported that it would be possible to operationally survive smaller impacts and avoid larger impacts, with meteors and space debris, and that the estimated cost of lifting a kilogram of payload to GEO and beyond would be $500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, Google X\'s Rapid Evaluation R&amp;D team began the design of a Space Elevator, eventually finding that no one had yet manufactured a perfectly formed carbon nanotube strand longer than a meter. They thus decided to put the project in "deep freeze" and also keep tabs on any advances in the carbon nanotube field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2018, researchers at Japan\'s <a href="/wiki/Shizuoka_University" title="Shizuoka University">Shizuoka University</a> launched STARS-Me, two <a href="/wiki/CubeSat" title="CubeSat">CubeSats</a> connected by a tether, which a mini-elevator will travel on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The prototype was launched as a test bed for a larger structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: In fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_elevators_in_fiction" title="Space elevators in fiction">Space elevators in fiction</a></div>\n<p>In 1979, space elevators were introduced to a broader audience with the simultaneous publication of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>\'s novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fountains_of_Paradise" title="The Fountains of Paradise">The Fountains of Paradise</a></i>, in which engineers construct a space elevator on top of a mountain peak in the fictional island country of "Taprobane" (loosely based on <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, albeit moved south to the Equator), and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sheffield" title="Charles Sheffield">Charles Sheffield</a>\'s first novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Web_Between_the_Worlds" title="The Web Between the Worlds">The Web Between the Worlds</a></i>, also featuring the building of a space elevator. Three years later, in <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Heinlein" title="Robert A. Heinlein">Robert A. Heinlein</a>\'s 1982 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_(novel)" title="Friday (novel)">Friday</a></i> the principal character makes use of the "Nairobi Beanstalk" in the course of her travels. In <a href="/wiki/Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson">Kim Stanley Robinson</a>\'s 1993 novel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Mars" title="Red Mars">Red Mars</a></i>, colonists build a space elevator on Mars that allows both for more colonists to arrive and also for natural resources mined there to be able to leave for Earth. In <a href="/wiki/David_Gerrold" title="David Gerrold">David Gerrold</a>\'s 2000 novel, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jumping_Off_The_Planet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jumping Off The Planet (page does not exist)">Jumping Off The Planet</a></i>, a family excursion up the Ecuador "beanstalk" is actually a child-custody kidnapping. Gerrold\'s book also examines some of the industrial applications of a mature elevator technology. In a biological version, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Slonczewski" title="Joan Slonczewski">Joan Slonczewski</a>\'s 2011 novel <i>The Highest Frontier</i> depicts a college student ascending a space elevator constructed of self-healing cables of anthrax bacilli. The engineered bacteria can regrow the cables when severed by space debris.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apparent_gravitational_field">Apparent gravitational field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Apparent gravitational field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>An Earth space elevator cable rotates along with the rotation of the Earth. Therefore the cable, and objects attached to it, would experience upward centrifugal force in the direction opposing the downward gravitational force. The higher up the cable the object is located, the less the gravitational pull of the Earth, and the stronger the upward centrifugal force due to the rotation, so that more centrifugal force opposes less gravity. The centrifugal force and the gravity are balanced at geosynchronous equatorial orbit (GEO). Above GEO, the centrifugal force is stronger than gravity, causing objects attached to the cable there to pull <i>upward</i> on it.\n</p><p>The net force for objects attached to the cable is called the <i>apparent gravitational field</i>.  The apparent gravitational field for attached objects is the (downward) gravity minus the (upward) centrifugal force. The apparent gravity experienced by an object on the cable is zero at GEO, downward below GEO, and upward above GEO.\n</p><p>The apparent gravitational field can be represented this way:<sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span> Ref<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aravind_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-aravind-41">[41]</a></sup> Table 1</span></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">The downward force of actual <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton\'s law of universal gravitation">gravity</a> <i>decreases</i> with height:  <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton\'s law of universal gravitation"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle g_{r}=-GM/r^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle g_{r}=-GM/r^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle g_{r}=-GM/r^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/11b7b6c8b732ce6028b6d47e056349076b9acbcf" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.524ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></a></div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">The upward <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> due to the planet\'s rotation <i>increases</i> with height: <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a=\\omega ^{2}r}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a=\\omega ^{2}r}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a=\\omega ^{2}r" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/760adabdbf29d51e7927c9213a435d75d5729f1f" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.877ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span></a></div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">Together, the apparent gravitational field is the sum of the two:\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle g=-{\\frac {GM}{r^{2}}}+\\omega ^{2}r}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle g=-{\\frac {GM}{r^{2}}}+\\omega ^{2}r}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle g=-{\\frac {GM}{r^{2}}}+\\omega ^{2}r}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c597ae5584d3dd6749cf0c49908afa9df45e9c5a" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:17.517ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></div></div>\n<p>where\n</p>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>g</i> is the acceleration of <i>apparent</i> gravity, pointing down (negative) or up (positive) along the vertical cable (m s<sup>&#8722;2</sup>),</div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>g<sub>r</sub></i> is the gravitational acceleration due to Earth\'s pull, pointing down (negative)(m s<sup>&#8722;2</sup>),</div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>a</i> is the centrifugal acceleration, pointing up (positive) along the vertical cable (m s<sup>&#8722;2</sup>),</div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>G</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a> (m<sup>3</sup> s<sup>&#8722;2</sup> kg<sup>&#8722;1</sup>)</div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>M</i> is the mass of the Earth (kg)</div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>r</i> is the distance from that point to Earth\'s center (m),</div>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><i>&#969;</i> is Earth\'s rotation speed (radian/s).</div>\n<p>At some point up the cable, the two terms (downward gravity and upward centrifugal force) are equal and opposite. Objects fixed to the cable at that point put no weight on the cable. This altitude (r<sub>1</sub>) depends on the mass of the planet and its rotation rate. Setting actual gravity equal to centrifugal acceleration gives:<sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span> Ref<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aravind_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-aravind-41">[41]</a></sup> page 126</span></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{1}=\\left({\\frac {GM}{\\omega ^{2}}}\\right)^{\\frac {1}{3}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{1}=\\left({\\frac {GM}{\\omega ^{2}}}\\right)^{\\frac {1}{3}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle r_{1}=\\left({\\frac {GM}{\\omega ^{2}}}\\right)^{\\frac {1}{3}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/97c3a9b608e87d5d40a8491fc7370a9e4bdd75a5" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:15.463ex; height:7.176ex;"/></span></div>\n<p>On Earth, this distance is 35,786&#160;km (22,236&#160;mi) above the surface, the altitude of geostationary orbit.<sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span> Ref<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aravind_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-aravind-41">[41]</a></sup> Table 1</span></sup>\n</p><p>On the cable <i>below</i> geostationary orbit, downward gravity would be greater than the upward centrifugal force, so the apparent gravity would pull objects attached to the cable downward.  Any object released from the cable below that level would initially accelerate downward along the cable.  Then gradually it would deflect eastward from the cable.  On the cable <i>above</i> the level of stationary orbit, upward centrifugal force would be greater than downward gravity, so the apparent gravity would pull objects attached to the cable <i>upward</i>.  Any object released from the cable <i>above</i> the geosynchronous level would initially accelerate <i>upward</i> along the cable.  Then gradually it would deflect westward from the cable.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cable_section">Cable section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cable section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Historically, the main technical problem has been considered the ability of the cable to hold up, with tension, the weight of itself below any given point. The greatest tension on a space elevator cable is at the point of geostationary orbit, 35,786&#160;km (22,236&#160;mi) above the Earth\'s equator. This means that the cable material, combined with its design, must be strong enough to hold up its own weight from the surface up to 35,786&#160;km (22,236&#160;mi). A cable which is thicker in cross section at that height than at the surface could better hold up its own weight over a longer length. How the cross section area tapers from the maximum at 35,786&#160;km (22,236&#160;mi) to the minimum at the surface is therefore an important design factor for a space elevator cable.\n</p><p>To maximize the usable excess strength for a given amount of cable material, the cable\'s cross section area would need to be designed for the most part in such a way that the <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a> (i.e., the tension per unit of cross sectional area) is constant along the length of the cable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aravind_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-aravind-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>  The constant-stress criterion is a starting point in the design of the cable cross section as it changes with altitude. Other factors considered in more detailed designs include thickening at altitudes where more space junk is present, consideration of the point stresses imposed by climbers, and the use of varied materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhaseII_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhaseII-43">[43]</a></sup>  To account for these and other factors, modern detailed cross section designs seek to achieve the largest <i><a href="/wiki/Factor_of_safety#Margin_of_safety" title="Factor of safety">safety margin</a></i> possible, with as little variation over altitude and time as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhaseII_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-PhaseII-43">[43]</a></sup>  In simple starting-point designs, that equates to constant-stress.\n</p><p>In the constant-stress case, the cross-section follows this differential equation:\n</p>\n<div style="padding-left: 3em; Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2012</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>To compare materials, the <i><a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">specific strength</a></i> of the material for the space elevator can be expressed in terms of the <i>characteristic length</i>, or "free breaking length": the length of an un-tapered cylindrical cable at which it will break under its own weight under constant gravity. For a given material, that length is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle L_{c}=\\sigma /(\\rho \\cdot g_{0})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; width:14.971ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>,\nwhere <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle \\sigma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle \\sigma }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle \\sigma }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ca336f17488923d11206e131f7eb2a569e8dde2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.202ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle g_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle g_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle g_{0}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/99c0877d2b2bdb19154a8cdd1f21e38b6cecd13e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.163ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> are as defined above.\nThe free breaking length needed is given by the equation\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta \\left[\\ln(S)\\right]=\\rho /\\sigma \\cdot g_{0}\\cdot r_{0}\\cdot (1+x/2-3/2\\cdot x^{1/3})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mo>\n<mn>3</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta \\left[\\ln(S)\\right]=\\rho /\\sigma \\cdot g_{0}\\cdot r_{0}\\cdot (1+x/2-3/2\\cdot x^{1/3})}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta\\left[ \\ln (S)\\right] = \\rho/\\sigma \\cdot g_0 \\cdot r_0 \\cdot ( 1 + x/2 - 3/2 \\cdot x^{1/3} )" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/16fd7ea228e54a86cb838682c81b67cc6b2b6c80" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex;
