--></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle x=w^{2}\\cdot r_{0}/g_{0}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="x = w^2 \\cdot r_0/g_0." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/714ddc02d71e886d18dbbf0e56be3169fa61f6c1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.901ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>\nIf one does not take into account the <i>x</i> factor (which reduces the strength needed by about 30 percent), this equation also says that the section ratio equals <i><a href="/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)" title="E (mathematical constant)">e</a></i> (or <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e^{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e^{1}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle e^{1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c7b21b094ee8f157b348c9ae99d6691385155c8c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.138ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>) when:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\sigma =\\rho \\cdot r_{0}\\cdot g_{0}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#961;<!-- &#961; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\sigma =\\rho \\cdot r_{0}\\cdot g_{0}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\sigma = \\rho \\cdot r_0 \\cdot g_0." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e99cca570058806f0107cd3c656746a347ee2897" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.901ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>If the material can support a free breaking length of only one tenth this, the section needed at a geosynchronous orbit will be <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\textstyle e^{10}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>10</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\textstyle e^{10}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\textstyle e^{10}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e2e81da639adaaecf478e6f187b63ebbb7a125c4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.96ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> (a factor of 22026.5) times the ground section.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg/220px-SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg/330px-SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg/440px-SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpaceElevatorClimbing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One concept for the space elevator has it tethered to a mobile seagoing platform.</div></div></div>\n<p>There are a variety of space elevator designs proposed for many planetary bodies. Almost every design includes a base station, a cable, climbers, and a counterweight. For an Earth Space Elevator the Earth\'s rotation creates upward <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> on the counterweight. The counterweight is held down by the cable while the cable is held up and taut by the counterweight. The base station anchors the whole system to the surface of the Earth. Climbers climb up and down the cable with cargo.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Base_station">Base station</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Base station">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Modern concepts for the base station/anchor are typically mobile stations, large oceangoing vessels or other mobile platforms. Mobile base stations would have the advantage over the earlier stationary concepts (with land-based anchors) by being able to maneuver to avoid high winds, storms, and <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>. Oceanic anchor points are also typically in <a href="/wiki/International_waters" title="International waters">international waters</a>, simplifying and reducing cost of negotiating territory use for the base station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Stationary land based platforms would have simpler and less costly logistical access to the base. They also would have an advantage of being able to be at high altitude, such as on top of mountains.  In an alternate concept, the base station could be a tower, forming a space elevator which comprises both a compression tower close to the surface, and a tether structure at higher altitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBIS1999_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-JBIS1999-11">[11]</a></sup> Combining a compression structure with a tension structure would reduce loads from the atmosphere at the Earth end of the tether, and reduce the distance into the Earth\'s gravity field the cable needs to extend, and thus reduce the critical strength-to-density requirements for the cable material, all other design factors being equal.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cable">Cable</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cable">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kohlenstoffnanoroehre_Animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Kohlenstoffnanoroehre_Animation.gif/170px-Kohlenstoffnanoroehre_Animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Kohlenstoffnanoroehre_Animation.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kohlenstoffnanoroehre_Animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes" title="Carbon nanotubes">Carbon nanotubes</a> are one of the candidates for a cable material</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg/170px-SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg/255px-SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg/340px-SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpaceElevatorAnchor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A seagoing anchor station would also act as a deep-water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaport" title="Seaport">seaport</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>A space elevator cable would need to carry its own weight as well as the additional weight of climbers. The required strength of the cable would vary along its length. This is because at various points it would have to carry the weight of the cable below, or provide a downward force to retain the cable and counterweight above. Maximum tension on a space elevator cable would be at geosynchronous altitude so the cable would have to be thickest there and taper carefully as it approaches Earth. Any potential cable design may be characterized by the taper factor &#8211; the ratio between the cable\'s radius at geosynchronous altitude and at the Earth\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA97029_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA97029-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The cable would need to be made of a material with a large <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">tensile strength/density ratio</a>. For example, the Edwards space elevator design assumes a cable material with a tensile strength of at least 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigapascal" title="Gigapascal">gigapascals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup>  Since Edwards consistently assumed the density of his carbon nanotube cable to be 1300 kg/m^3,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html-15">[15]</a></sup> that implies a specific strength of 77 megapascal/(kg/m^3). This value takes into consideration the entire weight of the space elevator. An untapered space elevator cable would need a material capable of sustaining a length of 4,960 kilometers (3,080&#160;mi) of its own weight <i>at <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a></i> to reach a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostationary" title="Geostationary">geostationary</a> altitude of 35,786&#160;km (22,236&#160;mi) without yielding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Therefore, a material with very high strength and lightness is needed.\n</p><p>For comparison, metals like titanium, steel or aluminium alloys have <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">breaking lengths</a> of only 20&#8211;30&#160;km. Modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man-made_fibers" title="Man-made fibers">fibre</a> materials such as <a href="/wiki/Kevlar" title="Kevlar">kevlar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibreglass" title="Fibreglass">fibreglass</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" title="Carbon fiber">carbon/graphite fibre</a> have breaking lengths of 100&#8211;400&#160;km. Nanoengineered materials such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes" title="Carbon nanotubes">carbon nanotubes</a> and, more recently discovered, <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a> ribbons (perfect two-dimensional sheets of carbon) are expected to have breaking lengths of 5000&#8211;6000&#160;km at sea level, and also are able to conduct electrical power.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>For a space elevator on Earth, with its comparatively high gravity, the cable material would need to be stronger and lighter than currently available materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huff.3353697_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huff.3353697-46">[46]</a></sup> For this reason, there has been a focus on the development of new materials that meet the demanding specific strength requirement. For high specific strength, carbon has advantages because it is only the 6th element in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. Carbon has comparatively few of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleons" title="Nucleons">protons and neutrons</a> which contribute most of the dead weight of any material. Most of the interatomic <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonding forces</a> of any element are contributed by only the <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">outer few</a> electrons. For carbon, the strength and stability of those bonds is high compared to the mass of the atom. The challenge in using carbon nanotubes remains to extend to macroscopic sizes the production of such material that are still perfect on the microscopic scale (as microscopic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystallographic_defects" title="Crystallographic defects">defects</a> are most responsible for material weakness).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huff.3353697_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Huff.3353697-46">[46]</a></sup>\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop.15185070_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pop.15185070-47">[47]</a></sup>\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extreme.176625_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-extreme.176625-48">[48]</a></sup> As of 2014, carbon nanotube technology allowed growing tubes up to a few tenths of meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanothread" title="Carbon nanothread">diamond nanothreads</a> were first synthesized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCIAM_DN_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCIAM_DN-5">[5]</a></sup>  Since they have strength properties similar to carbon nanotubes, diamond nanothreads were quickly seen as candidate cable material as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xtech_DN_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xtech_DN-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climbers">Climbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Climbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3208" data-file-width="2336" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg/170px-SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg/255px-SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg/340px-SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpaceElevatorInClouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A conceptual drawing of a space elevator climber ascending through the clouds.</div></div></div>\n<p>A space elevator cannot be an elevator in the typical sense (with moving cables) due to the need for the cable to be significantly wider at the center than at the tips. While various designs employing moving cables have been proposed, most cable designs call for the "elevator" to climb up a stationary cable.\n</p><p>Climbers cover a wide range of designs. On elevator designs whose cables are planar ribbons, most propose to use pairs of rollers to hold the cable with friction.\n</p><p>Climbers would need to be paced at optimal timings so as to minimize cable stress and oscillations and to maximize throughput. Lighter climbers could be sent up more often, with several going up at the same time. This would increase throughput somewhat, but would lower the mass of each individual payload.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LangGTOSS_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-LangGTOSS-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg/260px-Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg/390px-Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg/520px-Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_elevator_balance_of_forces--circular_Earth--more_accurate_force_vectors.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As the car climbs, the cable takes on a slight lean due to the Coriolis force.  The top of the cable travels faster than the bottom.  The climber is accelerated horizontally as it ascends by the Coriolis force which is imparted by angles of the cable.  The lean-angle shown is exaggerated.</div></div></div>\n<p>The horizontal speed, i.e. due to orbital rotation, of each part of the cable increases with altitude, proportional to distance from the center of the Earth, reaching low <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital speed</a> at a point approximately 66 percent of the height between the surface and geostationary orbit, or a height of about 23,400&#160;km. A payload released at this point would go into a highly eccentric elliptical orbit, staying just barely clear from atmospheric reentry, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">periapsis</a> at the same altitude as LEO and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoapsis" title="Apoapsis">apoapsis</a> at the release height. With increasing release height the orbit would become less eccentric as both periapsis and apoapsis increase, becoming circular at geostationary level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gassend_fall_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gassend_fall-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skyway_to_LEO_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skyway_to_LEO-52">[52]</a></sup>\nWhen the payload has reached GEO, the horizontal speed is exactly the speed of a circular orbit at that level, so that if released, it would remain adjacent to that point on the cable.  The payload can also continue climbing further up the cable beyond GEO, allowing it to obtain higher speed at jettison.  If released from 100,000&#160;km, the payload would have enough speed to reach the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhaseII_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-PhaseII-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a payload is lifted up a space elevator, it would gain not only altitude, but horizontal speed (angular momentum) as well. The angular momentum is taken from the Earth\'s rotation. As the climber ascends, it is initially moving slower than each successive part of cable it is moving on to. This is the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_force" title="Coriolis force">Coriolis force</a>: the climber "drags" (westward) on the cable, as it climbs, and slightly decreases the Earth\'s rotation speed. The opposite process would occur for descending payloads: the cable is tilted eastward, thus slightly increasing Earth\'s rotation speed.\n</p><p>The overall effect of the centrifugal force acting on the cable would cause it to constantly try to return to the energetically favorable vertical orientation, so after an object has been lifted on the cable, the counterweight would swing back toward the vertical like an inverted pendulum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LangGTOSS_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-LangGTOSS-50">[50]</a></sup> Space elevators and their loads would be designed so that the center of mass is always well-enough above the level of geostationary orbit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> to hold up the whole system. Lift and descent operations would need to be carefully planned so as to keep the pendulum-like motion of the counterweight around the tether point under control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Climber speed would be limited by the Coriolis force, available power, and by the need to ensure the climber\'s accelerating force does not break the cable. Climbers would also need to maintain a minimum average speed in order to move material up and down economically and expeditiously.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> At the speed of a very fast car or train of 300&#160;km/h (190&#160;mph) it will take about 5 days to climb to geosynchronous orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Powering_climbers">Powering climbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Powering climbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Both power and energy are significant issues for climbers&#8212;the climbers would need to gain a large amount of potential energy as quickly as possible to clear the cable for the next payload.\n</p><p>Various methods have been proposed to get that energy to the climber:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Transfer the energy to the climber through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_energy_transfer" title="Wireless energy transfer">wireless energy transfer</a> while it is climbing.</li>\n<li>Transfer the energy to the climber through some material structure while it is climbing.</li>\n<li>Store the energy in the climber before it starts &#8211; requires an extremely high <a href="/wiki/Specific_energy" title="Specific energy">specific energy</a> such as nuclear energy.</li>\n<li>Solar power &#8211; After the first 40&#160;km it is possible to use solar energy to power the climber<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Wireless energy transfer such as laser power beaming is currently considered the most likely method, using megawatt powered free electron or solid state lasers in combination with adaptive mirrors approximately 10&#160;m (33&#160;ft) wide and a photovoltaic array on the climber tuned to the laser frequency for efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup> For climber designs powered by power beaming, this efficiency is an important design goal. Unused energy would need to be re-radiated away with heat-dissipation systems, which add to weight.\n</p><p>Yoshio Aoki, a professor of precision machinery engineering at <a href="/wiki/Nihon_University" title="Nihon University">Nihon University</a> and director of the Japan Space Elevator Association, suggested including a second cable and using the conductivity of carbon nanotubes to provide power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapanUKTimes_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-JapanUKTimes-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Counterweight">Counterweight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Counterweight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_space_elev.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Nasa_space_elev.jpg/220px-Nasa_space_elev.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Nasa_space_elev.jpg/330px-Nasa_space_elev.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Nasa_space_elev.jpg/440px-Nasa_space_elev.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_space_elev.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Space Elevator with Space Station</div></div></div>\n<p>Several solutions have been proposed to act as a counterweight:\n</p>\n<ul><li>a heavy, captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASASci_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASASci-10">[10]</a></sup></li>\n<li>a <a href="/wiki/Space_dock" title="Space dock">space dock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> or <a href="/wiki/Spaceport" title="Spaceport">spaceport</a> positioned past geostationary orbit</li>\n<li>a further upward extension of the cable itself so that the net upward pull would be the same as an equivalent counterweight;</li>\n<li>parked spent climbers that had been used to thicken the cable during construction, other junk, and material lifted up the cable for the purpose of increasing the counterweight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhaseII_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-PhaseII-43">[43]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Extending the cable has the advantage of some simplicity of the task and the fact that a payload that went to the end of the counterweight-cable would acquire considerable velocity relative to the Earth, allowing it to be launched into interplanetary space. Its disadvantage is the need to produce greater amounts of cable material as opposed to using just anything available that has mass.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launching_into_deep_space">Launching into deep space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Launching into deep space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>An object attached to a space elevator at a radius of approximately 53,100&#160;km would be at <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> when released. Transfer orbits to the L1 and L2 <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a> could be attained by release at 50,630 and 51,240&#160;km, respectively, and transfer to lunar orbit from 50,960&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the end of Pearson\'s 144,000&#160;km (89,000&#160;mi) cable, the tangential velocity is 10.93 kilometers per second (6.79&#160;mi/s). That is more than enough to <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape</a> Earth\'s gravitational field and send probes at least as far out as  <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. Once at Jupiter, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_assist" title="Gravitational assist">gravitational assist</a> maneuver could permit solar escape velocity to be reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aravind_41-9"><a href="#cite_note-aravind-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_elevators">Extraterrestrial elevators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial elevators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A space elevator could also be constructed on other planets, asteroids and moons.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Martian</a> tether could be much shorter than one on Earth. Mars\' surface gravity is 38 percent of Earth\'s, while it rotates around its axis in about the same time as Earth. Because of this, Martian <a href="/wiki/Areostationary_orbit" title="Areostationary orbit">stationary orbit</a> is much closer to the surface, and hence the elevator could be much shorter. Current materials are already sufficiently strong to construct such an elevator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Building a Martian elevator would be complicated by the Martian moon <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a>, which is in a low orbit and intersects the Equator regularly (twice every orbital period of 11&#160;h 6&#160;min).\n</p><p>On the near side of the Moon, the strength-to-density required of the tether of a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_space_elevator" title="Lunar space elevator">lunar space elevator</a> exists in currently available materials. A lunar space elevator would be about 50,000 kilometers (31,000&#160;mi) long. Since the Moon does not rotate fast enough, there is no effective lunar-stationary orbit, but the <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a> could be used. The near side would extend through the Earth-Moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_lagrangian_point" title="Inner lagrangian point">L1</a> point from an anchor point near the center of the visible part of Earth\'s Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearson_2005_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson_2005-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the far side of the Moon, a lunar space elevator would need to be very long&#8212;more than twice the length of an Earth elevator&#8212;but due to the low gravity of the Moon, could also be made of existing engineering materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearson_2005_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson_2005-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rapidly spinning asteroids or moons could use cables to eject materials to convenient points, such as Earth orbits;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> or conversely, to eject materials to send a portion of the mass of the asteroid or moon to Earth orbit or a <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian point</a>. <a href="/wiki/Freeman_Dyson" title="Freeman Dyson">Freeman Dyson</a>, a physicist and mathematician, has suggested<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> using such smaller systems as power generators at points distant from the Sun where solar power is uneconomical.\n</p><p>A space elevator using presently available engineering materials could be constructed between mutually tidally locked worlds, such as <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> or the components of binary asteroid <a href="/wiki/90_Antiope" title="90 Antiope">90 Antiope</a>, with no terminus disconnect, according to Francis Graham of Kent State University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> However, spooled variable lengths of cable must be used due to ellipticity of the orbits.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator_construction" title="Space elevator construction">Space elevator construction</a></div>\n<p>The construction of a space elevator would need reduction of some technical risk. Some advances in engineering, manufacturing and physical technology are required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup> Once a first space elevator is built, the second one and all others would have the use of the previous ones to assist in construction, making their costs considerably lower. Such follow-on space elevators would also benefit from the great reduction in technical risk achieved by the construction of the first space elevator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to the work of Edwards in 2000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html-15">[15]</a></sup> most concepts for constructing a space elevator had the cable manufactured in space.  That was thought to be necessary for such a large and long object and for such a large counterweight.  Manufacturing the cable in space would be done in principle by using an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">Near-Earth object</a> for source material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMITHERMAN_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMITHERMAN-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>  These earlier concepts for construction require a large preexisting space-faring infrastructure to maneuver an asteroid into its needed orbit around Earth.  They also required the development of technologies for manufacture in space of large quantities of exacting materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISEC_SE_way_forward_2013_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISEC_SE_way_forward_2013-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since 2001, most work has focused on simpler methods of construction requiring much smaller space infrastructures.  They conceive the launch of a long cable on a large spool, followed by deployment of it in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-EDWARDS_PHASE_I_2000_472Edwards.html-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISEC_SE_way_forward_2013_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISEC_SE_way_forward_2013-64">[64]</a></sup>  The spool would be initially parked in a geostationary orbit above the planned anchor point. A long cable would be dropped "downward" (toward Earth) and would be balanced by a mass being dropped "upward" (away from Earth) for the whole system to remain on the geosynchronous orbit. Earlier designs imagined the balancing mass to be another cable (with counterweight) extending upward, with the main spool remaining at the original geosynchronous orbit level. Most current designs elevate the spool itself as the main cable is paid out, a simpler process. When the lower end of the cable is long enough to reach the surface of the Earth (at the equator), it would be anchored. Once anchored, the center of mass would be elevated more (by adding mass at the upper end or by paying out more cable). This would add more tension to the whole cable, which could then be used as an elevator cable.\n</p><p>One plan for construction uses conventional rockets to place a "minimum size" initial seed cable of only 19,800&#160;kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup> This first very small ribbon would be adequate to support the first 619&#160;kg climber. The first 207 climbers would carry up and attach more cable to the original, increasing its cross section area and widening the initial ribbon to about 160&#160;mm wide at its widest point. The result would be a 750-ton cable with a lift capacity of 20 tons per climber.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_issues_and_construction_challenges">Safety issues and construction challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Safety issues and construction challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator_safety" title="Space elevator safety">Space elevator safety</a></div>\n<p>For early systems, transit times from the surface to the level of geosynchronous orbit would be about five days. On these early systems, the time spent moving through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belts" title="Van Allen radiation belts">Van Allen radiation belts</a> would be enough that passengers would need to be protected from radiation by shielding, which would add mass to the climber and decrease payload.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstfloor_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-firstfloor-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A space elevator would present a navigational hazard, both to aircraft and spacecraft. Aircraft could be diverted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air-traffic_control" title="Air-traffic control">air-traffic control</a> restrictions. All objects in stable orbits that have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a> below the maximum altitude of the cable that are not synchronous with the cable would impact the cable eventually, unless avoiding action is taken. One potential solution proposed by Edwards is to use a movable anchor (a sea anchor) to allow the tether to "dodge" any space debris large enough to track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Impacts by space objects such as meteoroids, micrometeorites and orbiting man-made debris pose another design constraint on the cable. A cable would need to be designed to maneuver out of the way of debris, or absorb impacts of small debris without breaking.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator_economics" title="Space elevator economics">Space elevator economics</a></div>\n<p>With a space elevator, materials might be sent into orbit at a fraction of the current cost. As of 2000, conventional rocket designs cost about US$25,000 per <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> (US$11,000 per <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>) for transfer to geostationary orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>  Current space elevator proposals envision payload prices starting as low as $220 per kilogram ($100 per <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> similar to the $5&#8211;$300/kg estimates of the <a href="/wiki/Launch_loop" title="Launch loop">Launch loop</a>, but higher than the $310/ton to 500&#160;km orbit quoted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> to Dr. <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Pournelle" title="Jerry Pournelle">Jerry Pournelle</a> for an orbital airship system.\n</p><p>Philip Ragan, co-author of the book <i>Leaving the Planet by Space Elevator</i>, states that "The first country to deploy a space elevator will have a 95 percent cost advantage and could potentially control all space activities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="International_Space_Elevator_Consortium_.28ISEC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Space_Elevator_Consortium_(ISEC)">International Space Elevator Consortium (ISEC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: International Space Elevator Consortium (ISEC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The International Space Elevator Consortium (ISEC) is a US Non-Profit <a href="/wiki/501(c)(3)_organization" title="501(c)(3) organization">501(c)(3)</a> Corporation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> formed to promote the development, construction, and operation of a space elevator as "a revolutionary and efficient way to space for all humanity"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isec_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-isec-71">[71]</a></sup>.  It was formed after the Space Elevator Conference in <a href="/wiki/Redmond,_Washington" title="Redmond, Washington">Redmond, Washington</a> in July 2008 and became an affiliate organization with the <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Society" title="National Space Society">National Space Society</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> in August 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isec_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-isec-71">[71]</a></sup>.  ISEC hosts an annual Space Elevator conference at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle_Museum_of_Flight" title="Seattle Museum of Flight">Seattle Museum of Flight</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>.\n</p><p>ISEC coordinates with the two other major societies focusing on space elevators: the Japanese Space Elevator Association<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and EuroSpaceward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> ISEC supports symposia and presentations at the International Academy of Astronautics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and the International Astronautical Federation Congress<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> each year.  The organization published two issues of a peer-reviewed journal on space elevators called "CLIMB"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isec_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-isec-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and a magazine "Via Ad Astra"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>.\n</p><p>ISEC also conducts one-year studies focusing on individual topics. The process involves experts for one year of discussions on the topic of choice and culminates in a draft report that is presented and reviewed at the ISEC Space Elevator conference workshop to allow input from space elevator enthusiasts and other experts.  Study Reports are usually published early the following year, to date these are as follows&#160;: <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>2010 - Space Elevator Survivability, Space Debris Mitigation <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-36">[36]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2012 - Space Elevator Concept of Operations <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2013 - Design Consideration for Space Elevator Tether Climbers <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>,</li>\n<li>2014 - Space Elevator Architectures and Roadmaps <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2015 - Design Characteristics of a Space Elevator Earth Port <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2016 - Design Considerations for the Space Elevator Apex Anchor and GEO Node <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2017 - Design Considerations for a Software Space Elevator Simulator <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2018 - Design Considerations for the Multi-Stage Space Elevator <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_concepts">Related concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Related concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The conventional current concept of a "Space Elevator" has evolved from a static compressive structure reaching to the level of GEO, to the modern baseline idea of a static tensile structure anchored to the ground and extending to well above the level of GEO. In the current usage by practitioners (and in this article), a "Space Elevator" means the Tsiolkovsky-Artsutanov-Pearson type as considered by the International Space Elevator Consortium. This conventional type is a static structure fixed to the ground and extending into space high enough that cargo can climb the structure up from the ground to a level where simple release will put the cargo into an <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some concepts related to this modern baseline are not usually termed a "Space Elevator", but are similar in some way and are sometimes termed "Space Elevator" by their proponents. For example, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Moravec" title="Hans Moravec">Hans Moravec</a> published an article in 1977 called "A Non-Synchronous Orbital <a href="/wiki/Skyhook_(structure)" title="Skyhook (structure)">Skyhook</a>" describing a concept using a rotating cable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The rotation speed would exactly match the orbital speed in such a way that the tip velocity at the lowest point was zero compared to the object to be "elevated". It would dynamically grapple and then "elevate" high flying objects to orbit or low orbiting objects to higher orbit.\n</p><p>The original concept envisioned by Tsiolkovsky was a compression structure, a concept similar to an <a href="/wiki/Radio_masts_and_towers" title="Radio masts and towers">aerial mast</a>. While such structures might reach <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karman_line" title="Karman line">space</a> (100&#160;km, 62&#160;mi), they are unlikely to reach geostationary orbit. The concept of a Tsiolkovsky tower combined with a classic space elevator cable (reaching above the level of GEO) has been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBIS1999_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-JBIS1999-11">[11]</a></sup>  Other ideas use very tall compressive towers to reduce the demands on launch vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TorontoProposal_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-TorontoProposal-93">[93]</a></sup>  The vehicle is "elevated" up the tower, which may extend as high as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karman_line" title="Karman line">above the atmosphere</a>, and is launched from the top.  Such a tall tower to access near-space altitudes of 20&#160;km (12&#160;mi) has been proposed by various researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TorontoProposal_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-TorontoProposal-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landis1996_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-landis1996-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other concepts for <a href="/wiki/Non-rocket_spacelaunch" title="Non-rocket spacelaunch">non-rocket spacelaunch</a> related to a space elevator (or parts of a space elevator) include an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_ring" title="Orbital ring">orbital ring</a>, a pneumatic space tower,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Space_fountain" title="Space fountain">space fountain</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Launch_loop" title="Launch loop">launch loop</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Skyhook_(structure)" title="Skyhook (structure)">skyhook</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Space_tether" title="Space tether">space tether</a>, and a buoyant "SpaceShaft".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_elevator&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 25em; -webkit-column-width: 25em; column-width: 25em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.isec.org/index.php/what-is-a-space-elevator" rel="nofollow">"What is a Space Elevator?"</a>. The International Space Elevator Consortium. April 11, 2012.</cite><span class="Z3988"
